parity">PPP</a>)</span></th><td>2018&#160;estimate</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row"><div title="سودان.">سودان.</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Sudan</b> or <b>the Sudan</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/uː/: &#39;oo&#39; in &#39;goose&#39;">uː</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">السودان</span>&#8206; <i>as-Sūdān</i>), officially the <b>Republic of the Sudan</b><sup id="cite_ref-cia_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>  (Arabic: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">جمهورية السودان</span>&#8206; <i>Jumhūriyyat as-Sūdān</i>), is a country in Northeast <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>. It is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> to the north, the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> to the northeast, <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> to the east, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> to the southeast, <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> to the south, the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a> to the southwest, <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> to the west, and <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> to the northwest. It has a population of 43 million people (2018 estimate)<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> and occupies a total area of 1,886,068 square kilometres (728,215 square miles), making it the third-largest country in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Sudan's predominant religion is Islam,<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> and its official languages are <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>. The capital is <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a>, located at the confluence of the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile" title="Blue Nile">Blue</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">White Nile</a>. Since 2011, Sudan is the scene of <a href="/wiki/Sudanese_conflict_in_South_Kordofan_and_Blue_Nile" title="Sudanese conflict in South Kordofan and Blue Nile">ongoing military conflict in its regions South Kordofan and the Blue Nile</a>.
</p><p>Sudan's history goes back to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Pharaonic period</a>, witnessing the <a href="/wiki/Kerma_culture" title="Kerma culture">kingdom of Kerma</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2500 BC–1500 BC), the subsequent rule of the <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">Egyptian New Kingdom</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1500 BC–1070 BC) and the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">kingdom of Kush</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 785 BC–350 AD), which would in turn <a href="/wiki/Twenty-fifth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Twenty-fifth Dynasty of Egypt">control Egypt</a> itself for nearly a century. After the fall of Kush, the <a href="/wiki/Nubians" title="Nubians">Nubians</a> formed the three Christian kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Nobatia" title="Nobatia">Nobatia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Makuria" title="Makuria">Makuria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alodia" title="Alodia">Alodia</a>, with the latter two lasting until around 1500. Between the 14th and 15th centuries much of Sudan was settled by <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Arab nomads</a>. From the 16th–19th centuries, central and eastern Sudan were dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Sennar" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultanate of Sennar">Funj sultanate</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Darfur" title="Sultanate of Darfur">Darfur</a> ruled the west and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a> the far north. This period saw extensive <a href="/wiki/Islamisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamisation">Islamisation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabisation">Arabisation</a>.
</p><p>From 1820 to 1874 the entirety of Sudan  was conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_dynasty" title="Muhammad Ali dynasty">Muhammad Ali dynasty</a>. Between 1881 and 1885, the harsh Egyptian reign was eventually met with a successful <a href="/wiki/Mahdist_War" title="Mahdist War">revolt</a> led by the self-proclaimed <a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdi</a> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ahmad" title="Muhammad Ahmad">Muhammad Ahmad</a>, resulting in the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Mahdist_Sudan" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahdist Sudan">Caliphate of Omdurman</a>. This state was eventually destroyed in 1898 by the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">British</a>, who would then <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan" title="Anglo-Egyptian Sudan">govern Sudan together with Egypt</a>.
</p><p>The 20th century saw the growth of Sudanese nationalism and in 1953 Britain granted Sudan self-government. Independence was proclaimed on 1 January 1956. Since independence, Sudan has been ruled by a series of unstable parliamentary governments and military regimes. Under <a href="/wiki/Gaafar_Nimeiry" title="Gaafar Nimeiry">Gaafar Nimeiry</a>, Sudan instituted <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Islamic law</a> in 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-sudan.gov.sd_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sudan.gov.sd-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> This exacerbated the rift between the Islamic north, the seat of the government and the Animists and Christians in the <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">south</a>. Differences in language, religion, and political power erupted in a <a href="/wiki/Second_Sudanese_Civil_War" title="Second Sudanese Civil War">civil war</a> between government forces, strongly influenced by the <a href="/wiki/National_Islamic_Front" title="National Islamic Front">National Islamic Front</a> (NIF), and the southern rebels, whose most influential faction was the <a href="/wiki/Sudan_People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="Sudan People&#39;s Liberation Army">Sudan People's Liberation Army</a> (SPLA), eventually concluding in the independence of South Sudan in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The country's name <i>Sudan</i> is a name given to a <a href="/wiki/Sudan_(region)" title="Sudan (region)">geographical region</a> to the south of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>, stretching from Western Africa to eastern Central Africa. The name derives from the Arabic <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">bilād as-sūdān</i></i> (<span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">بلاد السودان</span>), or "the lands of the <a href="/wiki/Black_people" title="Black people">Blacks</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The name is one of several <a href="/wiki/Toponym" class="mw-redirect" title="Toponym">toponyms</a> sharing similar <a href="/wiki/Placename_etymology" class="mw-redirect" title="Placename etymology">etymologies</a>, ultimately meaning "land of the blacks" or similar meanings, in reference to the dark skin of the inhabitants. Initially, the term "Sudanese" had a negative connotation in Sudan due to its association with black African slaves. The idea of "Sudanese" nationalism goes back to the 1930s and 1940s, when it was popularised by young intellectuals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharkey200729–32_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharkey200729–32-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sudan" title="History of Sudan">History of Sudan</a></div>
<h3><span id="Prehistoric_Sudan_.28before_c._800_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric_Sudan_(before_c._800_BC)">Prehistoric Sudan (before c. 800 BC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistoric Sudan (before c. 800 BC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Western_Deffufa_-_Kerma.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Western_Deffufa_-_Kerma.jpg/220px-Western_Deffufa_-_Kerma.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Western_Deffufa_-_Kerma.jpg/330px-Western_Deffufa_-_Kerma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Western_Deffufa_-_Kerma.jpg/440px-Western_Deffufa_-_Kerma.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3504" data-file-height="2336" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Western_Deffufa_-_Kerma.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The large mud brick temple, known as the shrek or Western Deffufa, in the ancient city of <a href="/wiki/Kerma" title="Kerma">Kerma</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fortress_of_the_Middle_Kingdom,_reconstructed_under_the_New_Kingdom_(_about_1200_B.C.).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Fortress_of_the_Middle_Kingdom%2C_reconstructed_under_the_New_Kingdom_%28_about_1200_B.C.%29.jpg/220px-Fortress_of_the_Middle_Kingdom%2C_reconstructed_under_the_New_Kingdom_%28_about_1200_B.C.%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Fortress_of_the_Middle_Kingdom%2C_reconstructed_under_the_New_Kingdom_%28_about_1200_B.C.%29.jpg/330px-Fortress_of_the_Middle_Kingdom%2C_reconstructed_under_the_New_Kingdom_%28_about_1200_B.C.%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Fortress_of_the_Middle_Kingdom%2C_reconstructed_under_the_New_Kingdom_%28_about_1200_B.C.%29.jpg/440px-Fortress_of_the_Middle_Kingdom%2C_reconstructed_under_the_New_Kingdom_%28_about_1200_B.C.%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fortress_of_the_Middle_Kingdom,_reconstructed_under_the_New_Kingdom_(_about_1200_B.C.).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fortress of the Middle Kingdom, reconstructed under the New Kingdom (about 1200 B.C.)</div></div></div>
<p>By the eighth millennium BC, people of a <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> culture had settled into a sedentary way of life  there in fortified <a href="/wiki/Mudbrick" title="Mudbrick">mudbrick</a> villages, where they supplemented hunting and fishing on the Nile with <a href="/wiki/Grain" title="Grain">grain</a> gathering and <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> herding.<sup id="cite_ref-locearlyhist_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-locearlyhist-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> During the fifth millennium BC, migrations from the drying Sahara brought neolithic people into the Nile Valley along with agriculture. The population that resulted from this cultural and genetic mixing developed a social hierarchy over the next centuries which became the Kingdom of Kush (with the capital at Kerma) at 1700 BC. Anthropological and archaeological research indicate that during the predynastic period Nubia and Nagadan Upper Egypt were ethnically, and culturally nearly identical, and thus, simultaneously evolved systems of pharaonic kingship by 3300 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Keita,_S.O.Y._1993_129–54_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keita,_S.O.Y._1993_129–54-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Kingdom_of_Kush_.28c._800_BC.E2.80.93350_AD.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Kush_(c._800_BC–350_AD)">Kingdom of Kush (c. 800 BC–350 AD)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Kush (c. 800 BC–350 AD)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kingdom of Kush</a> and <a href="/wiki/Twenty-fifth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Twenty-fifth Dynasty of Egypt">Twenty-fifth Dynasty of Egypt</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sudan_Meroe_Pyramids_2001.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Sudan_Meroe_Pyramids_2001.JPG/250px-Sudan_Meroe_Pyramids_2001.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Sudan_Meroe_Pyramids_2001.JPG/375px-Sudan_Meroe_Pyramids_2001.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Sudan_Meroe_Pyramids_2001.JPG/500px-Sudan_Meroe_Pyramids_2001.JPG 2x" data-file-width="591" data-file-height="403" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sudan_Meroe_Pyramids_2001.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nubian_pyramids" title="Nubian pyramids">Nubian pyramids</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mero%C3%AB" title="Meroë">Meroë</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Xerxes_detail_Ethiopian.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Xerxes_detail_Ethiopian.jpg/250px-Xerxes_detail_Ethiopian.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Xerxes_detail_Ethiopian.jpg/375px-Xerxes_detail_Ethiopian.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Xerxes_detail_Ethiopian.jpg/500px-Xerxes_detail_Ethiopian.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1150" data-file-height="983" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Xerxes_detail_Ethiopian.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Kušiya</i> soldier of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_army" class="mw-redirect" title="Achaemenid army">Achaemenid army</a>, circa 480 BCE. <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a> tomb relief.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kingdom of Kush</a> was an ancient <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubian</a> state centered on the confluences of the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile" title="Blue Nile">Blue Nile</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">White Nile</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Atbarah_River" title="Atbarah River">Atbarah River</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile River</a>. It was established after the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> collapse and the disintegration of the <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">New Kingdom of Egypt</a>, centered at Napata in its early phase.
</p><p>After King <a href="/wiki/Kashta" title="Kashta">Kashta</a> ("the Kushite") invaded Egypt in the eighth century BC, the Kushite kings ruled as pharaohs of the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-fifth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Twenty-fifth Dynasty of Egypt">Twenty-fifth Dynasty of Egypt</a> for a century before being defeated and driven out by the <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrians</a>. At the height of their glory, the Kushites conquered an empire that stretched from what is now known as <a href="/wiki/South_Kordofan" title="South Kordofan">South Kordofan</a> all the way to the Sinai. Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Piye" title="Piye">Piye</a> attempted to expand the empire into the Near East, but was thwarted by the Assyrian king <a href="/wiki/Sargon_II" title="Sargon II">Sargon II</a>.
</p><p>The Kingdom of Kush is mentioned in the Bible as having saved the Israelites from the wrath of the Assyrians, although disease among the besiegers was the main reason for the failure to take the city.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (June 2013)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
The war that took place between Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Taharqa" title="Taharqa">Taharqa</a> and the Assyrian king <a href="/wiki/Sennacherib" title="Sennacherib">Sennacherib</a> was a decisive event in western history, with the Nubians being defeated in their attempts to gain a foothold in the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> by Assyria. Sennacherib's successor <a href="/wiki/Esarhaddon" title="Esarhaddon">Esarhaddon</a> went further, and invaded Egypt itself, deposing Taharqa and driving the Nubians from Egypt entirely. Taharqa fled back to his homeland where he died two years later. Egypt became an Assyrian colony; however, king <a href="/wiki/Tantamani" title="Tantamani">Tantamani</a>, after succeeding Taharqa, made a final determined attempt to regain Egypt. Esarhaddon died while preparing to leave the Assyrian capital of <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a> in order to eject him. However, his successor <a href="/wiki/Ashurbanipal" title="Ashurbanipal">Ashurbanipal</a> (668 – c. 627 BC) sent a large army into southern Egypt and routed Tantamani, ending all hopes of a revival of the Nubian Empire.
</p><p>During Classical Antiquity, the Nubian capital was at <a href="/wiki/Mero%C3%AB" title="Meroë">Meroë</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greek</a> geography, the Meroitic kingdom was known as <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> (a term also used earlier by the Assyrians when encountering the Nubians). The civilisation of Kush was among the first in the world to use iron smelting technology. The Nubian kingdom at Meroë persisted until the mid fourth century AD.
</p>
<h3><span id="Medieval_Christian_Nubian_kingdoms_.28c._350.E2.80.931500.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_Christian_Nubian_kingdoms_(c._350–1500)">Medieval Christian Nubian kingdoms (c. 350–1500)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Medieval Christian Nubian kingdoms (c. 350–1500)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Nobatia" title="Nobatia">Nobatia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Makuria" title="Makuria">Makuria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alodia" title="Alodia">Alodia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Daju_kingdom" title="Daju kingdom">Daju kingdom</a></div> 
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Christian_Nubia.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Christian_Nubia.png/200px-Christian_Nubia.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Christian_Nubia.png/300px-Christian_Nubia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Christian_Nubia.png/400px-Christian_Nubia.png 2x" data-file-width="499" data-file-height="649" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Christian_Nubia.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The three Christian Nubian kingdoms. The northern border of <a href="/wiki/Alodia" title="Alodia">Alodia</a> is unclear, but it also might have been located further north, between the fourth and fifth <a href="/wiki/Cataracts_of_the_Nile" title="Cataracts of the Nile">Nile cataract</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelsby200226_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelsby200226-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>On the turn of the fifth century the <a href="/wiki/Blemmyes" title="Blemmyes">Blemmyes</a> established a short-lived state in Upper Egypt and Lower Nubia, probably centered around Talmis (<a href="/wiki/Kalabsha" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalabsha">Kalabsha</a>), but before 450 they were already driven out of the Nile Valley by the Nobatians. The latter eventually founded a kingdom on their own, <a href="/wiki/Nobatia" title="Nobatia">Nobatia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelsby200216–22_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelsby200216–22-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
By the 6th century there were in total three Nubian kingdoms: Nobatia in the north, which had its capital at Pachoras (<a href="/wiki/Faras" title="Faras">Faras</a>); the central kingdom, <a href="/wiki/Makuria" title="Makuria">Makuria</a> centred at Tungul (<a href="/wiki/Old_Dongola" title="Old Dongola">Old Dongola</a>), about 13 kilometres (8 miles) south of modern <a href="/wiki/Dongola" title="Dongola">Dongola</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Alodia" title="Alodia">Alodia</a>, in the heartland of the old Kushitic kingdom, which had its capital at <a href="/wiki/Soba_(city)" title="Soba (city)">Soba</a> (now a suburb of modern-day Khartoum).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelsby200224,_26_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelsby200224,_26-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Still in the sixth century they converted to Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelsby200216–17_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelsby200216–17-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In the seventh century, probably at some point between 628 and 642, Nobatia was incorporated into Makuria.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWerner201377_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWerner201377-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 639 and 641 the Muslim Arabs of the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a> <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Muslim conquest of Egypt">conquered</a> <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> Egypt. In <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Dongola" title="First Battle of Dongola">641 or 642</a> and again in <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Dongola" title="Second Battle of Dongola">652</a> they invaded Nubia but were repelled, making the Nubians one of the few who managed to defeat the Arabs during the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_expansion" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic expansion">Islamic expansion</a>. Afterwards the Makurian king and the Arabs agreed on a <a href="/wiki/Baqt" title="Baqt">unique non-aggression pact that also included an annual exchange of gifts</a>, thus acknowledging Makuria's independence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelsby200268–70_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelsby200268–70-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> While the Arabs failed to conquer Nubia they began to settle east of the Nile, where they eventually founded several port towns<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHasan196731_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHasan196731-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and intermarried with the local <a href="/wiki/Beja_people" title="Beja people">Beja</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelsby200277–78_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelsby200277–78-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Moses_George_of_Makuria.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/King_Moses_George_of_Makuria.jpg/220px-King_Moses_George_of_Makuria.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/King_Moses_George_of_Makuria.jpg/330px-King_Moses_George_of_Makuria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/King_Moses_George_of_Makuria.jpg/440px-King_Moses_George_of_Makuria.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2552" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Moses_George_of_Makuria.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moses_George_of_Makuria&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Moses George of Makuria (page does not exist)">Moses George</a>, king of Makuria and Alodia</div></div></div>
<p>From the mid 8th-mid 11th century the political power and cultural development of Christian Nubia peaked.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShinnie1978572_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShinnie1978572-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In 747 Makuria invaded Egypt, which at this time belonged to the declining <a href="/wiki/Umayyads" class="mw-redirect" title="Umayyads">Umayyads</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWerner201384_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWerner201384-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and it did so again in the early 960s, when it pushed as far north as <a href="/wiki/Akhmim" title="Akhmim">Akhmim</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWerner2013101_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWerner2013101-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Makuria maintained close dynastic ties with Alodia, perhaps resulting in the temporary unification of the two kingdoms into one state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelsby200289_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelsby200289-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The culture of the medieval Nubians has been described as "<i>Afro-Byzantine</i>",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERuffini2012264_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERuffini2012264-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> but was also increasingly influenced by Arab culture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartens-Czarnecka2015249–265_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartens-Czarnecka2015249–265-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The state organisation was extremely centralised,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWerner2013254_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWerner2013254-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> being based on the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_bureaucracy" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine bureaucracy">Byzantine bureaucracy</a> of the 6th and 7th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards2004237_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards2004237-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Arts flourished in the form of pottery paintings<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams1977496_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams1977496-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and especially wall paintings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams1977482_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams1977482-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The Nubians developed an own alphabet for their language, <a href="/wiki/Old_Nubian" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Nubian">Old Nobiin</a>, basing it on the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_alphabet" title="Coptic alphabet">Coptic alphabet</a>, while also utilizing <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek" title="Medieval Greek">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelsby2002236–239_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelsby2002236–239-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Women enjoyed high social status: they had access to education, could own, buy and sell land and often used their wealth to endow churches and church paintings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWerner2013344–345_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWerner2013344–345-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Even the royal succession was <a href="/wiki/Matrilineal" class="mw-redirect" title="Matrilineal">matrilineal</a>, with the son of the king's sister being the rightful heir.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelsby200288_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelsby200288-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From the late 11th/12th century, Makuria's capital Dongola was in decline, and Alodia's capital declined in the 12th century as well.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelsby2002252_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelsby2002252-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In the 14th and 15th centuries <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> tribes overran most of Sudan,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHasan1967176_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHasan1967176-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> migrating to the <a href="/wiki/Butana" title="Butana">Butana</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gezira_(state)" title="Gezira (state)">Gezira</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kordofan" title="Kordofan">Kordofan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Darfur" title="Darfur">Darfur</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHasan1967145_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHasan1967145-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> In 1365 a civil war forced the Makurian court to flee to <a href="/w/index.php?title=Gebel_Adda&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Gebel Adda (page does not exist)">Gebel Adda</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lower_Nubia" title="Lower Nubia">Lower Nubia</a>, while Dongola was destroyed and left to the Arabs. Afterwards Makuria continued to exist only as a petty kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWerner2013143–145_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWerner2013143–145-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> After the prosperous<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELajtar2011130–131_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELajtar2011130–131-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> reign of king <a href="/wiki/Joel_of_Dotawo" title="Joel of Dotawo">Joel</a> (<abbr title="floruit (&quot;flourished&quot; –&#160;known to have been active at a particular time or during a particular period)">fl.</abbr> 1463–1484) Makuria probably collapsed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERuffini2012256_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERuffini2012256-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> 
To the south, the kingdom of Alodia fell to either the Arabs, commanded by tribal leader <a href="/w/index.php?title=Abdallah_Jamma&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Abdallah Jamma (page does not exist)">Abdallah Jamma</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Funj" class="mw-redirect" title="Funj">Funj</a>, an African people originating from the south.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelsby2002255_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelsby2002255-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Datings range from the <a href="/wiki/Hijri_year" title="Hijri year">9th century after the Hijra</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1396–1494),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVantini1975786–787_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVantini1975786–787-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> the late 15th century,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHasan1967133_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHasan1967133-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> 1504<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVantini1975784_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVantini1975784-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> to 1509.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVantini2006487–489_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVantini2006487–489-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> An alodian rump state might have survived in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Fazughli" title="Kingdom of Fazughli">kingdom of Fazughli</a>, lasting until 1685.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpaulding197412–30_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpaulding197412–30-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Islamic_kingdoms_of_Sennar_and_Darfur_.28c._1500.E2.80.931821.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_kingdoms_of_Sennar_and_Darfur_(c._1500–1821)">Islamic kingdoms of Sennar and Darfur (c. 1500–1821)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Islamic kingdoms of Sennar and Darfur (c. 1500–1821)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Sennar" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultanate of Sennar">Sultanate of Sennar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunjur_kingdom" title="Tunjur kingdom">Tunjur kingdom</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Darfur" title="Sultanate of Darfur">Sultanate of Darfur</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sennar_mosque_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Sennar_mosque_%28cropped%29.jpg/270px-Sennar_mosque_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="270" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Sennar_mosque_%28cropped%29.jpg/405px-Sennar_mosque_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4d/Sennar_mosque_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="473" data-file-height="311" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sennar_mosque_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The great mosque of <a href="/wiki/Sennar" title="Sennar">Sennar</a>, built in the 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoltDaly200025_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoltDaly200025-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 1504 the Funj are recorded to have founded the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sennar" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Sennar">Kingdom of Sennar</a>, in which Abdallah Jamma's realm was incorporated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197425–26_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197425–26-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> By 1523, when Jewish traveller <a href="/wiki/David_Reubeni" title="David Reubeni">David Reubeni</a> visited Sudan, the Funj state already extended as far north as Dongola.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197426_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197426-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, Islam began to be preached on the Nile by <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> holymen who settled there in the 15th and 16th centuries<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoimeier2013150_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoimeier2013150-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> and by David Reubeni's visit king <a href="/wiki/Amara_Dunqas" title="Amara Dunqas">Amara Dunqas</a>, previously a Pagan or nominal Christian, was recorded to be Muslim.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197431_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197431-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> However, the Funj would retain un-Islamic customs like the divine kingship or the consummation of alcohol until the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoimeier2013151–152_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoimeier2013151–152-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Sudanese <a href="/wiki/Folk_religion" title="Folk religion">folk Islam</a> preserved many rituals stemming from Christian traditions until the recent past.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWerner2013177–184_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWerner2013177–184-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Soon the Funj came in conflict with the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a>, who had occupied <a href="/wiki/Suakin" title="Suakin">Suakin</a> around 1526<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeacock201298_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeacock201298-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> and eventually pushed south along the Nile, reaching the third Nile cataract area in 1583/1584. A subsequent Ottoman attempt to capture Dongola was <a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Hannik&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Battle of Hannik (page does not exist)">repelled</a> by the Funj in 1585.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeacock201296–97_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeacock201296–97-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Afterwards, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannik&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hannik (page does not exist)">Hannik</a>, located just south of the third cataract, would mark the border between the two states.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197435_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197435-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The aftermath of the Ottoman invasion saw the attempted usurpation of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ajib_the_Great&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ajib the Great (page does not exist)">Ajib</a>, a minor king of northern Nubia. While the Funj eventually killed him in 1611/1612 his successors, the <a href="/wiki/Abdallabi_tribe" title="Abdallabi tribe">Abdallab</a>, were granted to govern everything north of the confluence of Blue and White Niles with considerable autonomy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197436–40_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197436–40-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 17th century the Funj state reached its widest extent,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams1977601_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams1977601-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> but in the following century it began to decline.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197478_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197478-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> A coup in 1718 brought a dynastic change,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197488_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197488-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> while another one in 1761–1762<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpaulding197424-25_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpaulding197424-25-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Hamaj_Regency" title="Hamaj Regency">Hamaj regency</a>, where the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamaj&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hamaj (page does not exist)">Hamaj</a> (a people from the Ethiopian borderlands) effectively ruled while the Funj sultans were their mere puppets.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197494–95_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197494–95-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Shortly afterwards the sultanate began to fragment;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197498_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197498-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> by the early 19th century it was essentially restricted to the Gezira.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpaulding1985382_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpaulding1985382-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Southern_Sudan_-_1800.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Southern_Sudan_-_1800.png/260px-Southern_Sudan_-_1800.png" decoding="async" width="260" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Southern_Sudan_-_1800.png/390px-Southern_Sudan_-_1800.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Southern_Sudan_-_1800.png/520px-Southern_Sudan_-_1800.png 2x" data-file-width="717" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Southern_Sudan_-_1800.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Southern Sudan in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1800</div></div></div>
<p>The coup of 1718 kicked off a policy of pursuing a more orthodox Islam, which in turn promoted the <a href="/wiki/Arabisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabisation">Arabisation</a> of the state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoimeier2013152_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoimeier2013152-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In order to legitimise their rule over their Arab subjects the Funj began to propagate an <a href="/wiki/Banu_Umayya" class="mw-redirect" title="Banu Umayya">Umayyad descend</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpaulding1985210–212_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpaulding1985210–212-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> North of the confluence of the Blue and White Niles, as far downstream as <a href="/wiki/Al_Dabbah,_Sudan" title="Al Dabbah, Sudan">Al Dabbah</a>, the Nubians adopted the tribal identity of the Arab <a href="/wiki/Ja%27alin_tribe" title="Ja&#39;alin tribe">Jaalin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams1977557–558_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams1977557–558-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Until the 19th century Arabic had succeeded in becoming the dominant language of central riverine Sudan<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards2004260_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards2004260-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197428–29_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding197428–29-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHesse200250_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHesse200250-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> and most of Kordofan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHesse200221–22_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHesse200221–22-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>West of the Nile, in <a href="/wiki/Darfur" title="Darfur">Darfur</a>, the Islamic period saw at first the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Tunjur_kingdom" title="Tunjur kingdom">Tunjur kingdom</a>, which replaced the old <a href="/wiki/Daju_kingdom" title="Daju kingdom">Daju kingdom</a> in the 15th century<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGregor2011Table_1_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGregor2011Table_1-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and extended as far west as <a href="/wiki/Wadai_Empire" title="Wadai Empire">Wadai</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding1974110_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding1974110-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tunjur_people" title="Tunjur people">Tunjur people</a> were probably Arabised <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berbers</a> and, their ruling elite at least, Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGregor2011132_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGregor2011132-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In the  17th century the Tunjur were driven from power by the <a href="/wiki/Fur_people" title="Fur people">Fur</a> <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Darfur" title="Sultanate of Darfur">Keira sultanate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding1974110_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding1974110-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The Keira state, nominally Muslim since the reign of <a href="/wiki/Sulayman_Solong" title="Sulayman Solong">Sulayman Solong</a> (r. <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1660–1680),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding1974123_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding1974123-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> was initially a small kingdom in northern <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Marra" class="mw-redirect" title="Jebel Marra">Jebel Marra</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoltDaly200031_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoltDaly200031-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> but expanded west- and northwards in the early 18th century<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding1974126_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheySpaulding1974126-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> and eastwards under the rule of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_Tayrab_of_Darfur&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Muhammad Tayrab of Darfur (page does not exist)">Muhammad Tayrab</a> (r. 1751–1786),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheyTubiana20079_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheyTubiana20079-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> peaking in the conquest of Kordofan in 1785.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheyTubiana20072_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheyTubiana20072-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The apogee of this empire, now roughly the size of present-day <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheyTubiana20072_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheyTubiana20072-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> would last until 1821.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheyTubiana20079_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;FaheyTubiana20079-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Turkiyah_and_Mahdist_Sudan_.281821.E2.80.931899.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Turkiyah_and_Mahdist_Sudan_(1821–1899)">Turkiyah and Mahdist Sudan (1821–1899)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Turkiyah and Mahdist Sudan (1821–1899)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sudan_(1821%E2%80%931885)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Sudan (1821–1885)">History of Sudan (1821–1885)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mahdist_Sudan" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahdist Sudan">Mahdist Sudan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Egyptian_conquest_of_Sudan" title="Anglo-Egyptian conquest of Sudan">Anglo-Egyptian conquest of Sudan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ismail_Pacha.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Ismail_Pacha.JPG/180px-Ismail_Pacha.JPG" decoding="async" width="180" height="297" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Ismail_Pacha.JPG/270px-Ismail_Pacha.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7a/Ismail_Pacha.JPG 2x" data-file-width="273" data-file-height="450" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ismail_Pacha.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ismail Pasha, the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> <a href="/wiki/Khedive" title="Khedive">Khedive</a> of Egypt and Sudan from 1863 to 1879.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Ahmad_al-Mahdi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Muhammad_Ahmad_al-Mahdi.jpg/180px-Muhammad_Ahmad_al-Mahdi.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Muhammad_Ahmad_al-Mahdi.jpg/270px-Muhammad_Ahmad_al-Mahdi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Muhammad_Ahmad_al-Mahdi.jpg/360px-Muhammad_Ahmad_al-Mahdi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="724" data-file-height="928" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Ahmad_al-Mahdi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ahmad" title="Muhammad Ahmad">Muhammad Ahmad</a>, ruler of Sudan (1881–1885).</div></div></div>
<p>In 1821, the Ottoman ruler of Egypt, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Muhammad Ali of Egypt</a>, had invaded and conquered northern Sudan. Although technically the <a href="/wiki/Vali_(governor)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vali (governor)">Vali</a> of Egypt under the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, Muhammad Ali styled himself as <a href="/wiki/Khedive" title="Khedive">Khedive</a> of a virtually independent Egypt. Seeking to add Sudan to his domains, he sent his third son Ismail (not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Isma%27il_Pasha" title="Isma&#39;il Pasha">Isma'il Pasha</a> mentioned later) to conquer the country, and subsequently incorporate it into Egypt. With the exception of the Shaiqiya and the Darfur sultanate in Kordofan, he was met without resistance. The Egyptian policy of conquest was expanded and intensified by <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Pasha_of_Egypt" title="Ibrahim Pasha of Egypt">Ibrahim Pasha</a>'s son, Isma'il, under whose reign most of the remainder of modern-day Sudan was conquered.
</p><p>The Egyptian authorities made significant improvements to the Sudanese infrastructure (mainly in the north), especially with regard to irrigation and cotton production. In 1879, the <a href="/wiki/International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)" title="International relations of the Great Powers (1814–1919)">Great Powers</a> forced the removal of Ismail and established his son <a href="/wiki/Tewfik_Pasha" title="Tewfik Pasha">Tewfik Pasha</a> in his place. Tewfik's corruption and mismanagement resulted in the <a href="/wiki/%27Urabi_revolt" title="&#39;Urabi revolt">'Urabi revolt</a>, which threatened the Khedive's survival. Tewfik appealed for help to the British, who subsequently occupied Egypt in 1882. Sudan was left in the hands of the Khedivial government, and the mismanagement and corruption of its officials.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SlatinWingate1896_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SlatinWingate1896-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Khedivial period, dissent had spread due to harsh taxes imposed on most activities. Taxation on irrigation wells and farming lands were so high most farmers abandoned their farms and livestock. During the 1870s, European initiatives against the <a href="/wiki/Slave_trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a> had an adverse impact on the economy of northern Sudan, precipitating the rise of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ahmad" title="Muhammad Ahmad">Mahdist</a> forces.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ahmad" title="Muhammad Ahmad">Muhammad Ahmad ibn Abd Allah</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdi</a></i> (Guided One), offered to the <i>ansars</i> (his followers) and those who surrendered to him a choice between adopting Islam or being killed. The Mahdiyah (Mahdist regime) imposed traditional Sharia <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Islamic laws</a>.
</p><p>From his announcement of the Mahdiyya in June 1881 until the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Khartoum" title="Siege of Khartoum">fall of Khartoum</a> in January 1885, Muhammad Ahmad led a <a href="/wiki/Mahdist_War" title="Mahdist War">successful military campaign</a> against the Turco-Egyptian government of the Sudan, known as the <a href="/wiki/Turkiyah" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkiyah">Turkiyah</a>. Muhammad Ahmad died on 22 June 1885, a mere six months after the conquest of Khartoum. After a power struggle amongst his deputies, <a href="/wiki/Abdallahi_ibn_Muhammad" title="Abdallahi ibn Muhammad">Abdallahi ibn Muhammad</a>, with the help primarily of the <a href="/wiki/Baggara" title="Baggara">Baggara</a> of western Sudan, overcame the opposition of the others and emerged as unchallenged leader of the Mahdiyah. After consolidating his power, Abdallahi ibn Muhammad assumed the title of <i>Khalifa</i> (successor) of the Mahdi, instituted an administration, and appointed <a href="/wiki/Ansar_(Sudan)" title="Ansar (Sudan)">Ansar</a> (who were usually <a href="/wiki/Baggara" title="Baggara">Baggara</a>) as emirs over each of the several provinces.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Omdurman-1.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Battle_of_Omdurman-1.JPG/250px-Battle_of_Omdurman-1.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Battle_of_Omdurman-1.JPG/375px-Battle_of_Omdurman-1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Battle_of_Omdurman-1.JPG/500px-Battle_of_Omdurman-1.JPG 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="530" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Omdurman-1.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The flight of the Khalifa after his defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Omdurman" title="Battle of Omdurman">Battle of Omdurman</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Regional relations remained tense throughout much of the Mahdiyah period, largely because of the Khalifa's brutal methods to extend his rule throughout the country. In 1887, a 60,000-man Ansar army invaded <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, penetrating as far as <a href="/wiki/Gondar" title="Gondar">Gondar</a>. In March 1889, king <a href="/wiki/Yohannes_IV" title="Yohannes IV">Yohannes IV</a> of Ethiopia marched on <a href="/wiki/Metemma" title="Metemma">Metemma</a>; however, after Yohannes fell in battle, the Ethiopian forces withdrew. Abd ar Rahman an Nujumi, the Khalifa's general, attempted an invasion of Egypt in 1889, but British-led Egyptian troops defeated the Ansar at Tushkah. The failure of the Egyptian invasion broke the spell of the Ansar's invincibility. The <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgians</a> prevented the Mahdi's men from conquering <a href="/wiki/Equatoria" title="Equatoria">Equatoria</a>, and in 1893, the Italians repelled an Ansar attack at <a href="/wiki/Agordat" title="Agordat">Agordat</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>) and forced the Ansar to withdraw from Ethiopia.
</p><p>In the 1890s, the British sought to re-establish their control over Sudan, once more officially in the name of the Egyptian Khedive, but in actuality treating the country as a British colony. By the early 1890s, British, French and Belgian claims had converged at the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> headwaters. Britain feared that the other powers would take advantage of Sudan's instability to acquire territory previously annexed to Egypt. Apart from these political considerations, Britain wanted to establish control over the Nile to safeguard a planned irrigation dam at <a href="/wiki/Aswan" title="Aswan">Aswan</a>. <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Kitchener" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbert Kitchener">Herbert Kitchener</a> led military campaigns against the <a href="/wiki/Mahdist_Sudan" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahdist Sudan">Mahdist Sudan</a> from 1896 to 1898. Kitchener's campaigns culminated in a decisive victory in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Omdurman" title="Battle of Omdurman">Battle of Omdurman</a> on 2 September 1898.
</p>
<h3><span id="Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan_.281899.E2.80.931956.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan_(1899–1956)">Anglo-Egyptian Sudan (1899–1956)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Anglo-Egyptian Sudan (1899–1956)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan" title="Anglo-Egyptian Sudan">Anglo-Egyptian Sudan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg/240px-The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg/360px-The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg/480px-The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="764" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Mahdist_War" title="Mahdist War">Mahdist War</a> was fought between a group of Muslim dervishes, called <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ahmad" title="Muhammad Ahmad">Mahdists</a>, who had over-run much of Sudan, and the British forces.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1899, Britain and Egypt reached an agreement under which Sudan was run by a governor-general appointed by Egypt with British consent.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In reality, Sudan was effectively administered as a <a href="/wiki/Crown_colony" title="Crown colony">Crown colony</a>. The British were keen to reverse the process, started under <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Muhammad Ali Pasha</a>, of uniting the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Nile Valley">Nile Valley</a> under Egyptian leadership and sought to frustrate all efforts aimed at further uniting the two countries.
</p><p>Under the Delimitation, Sudan's border with Abyssinia was contested by raiding tribesmen trading slaves, breaching boundaries of the law.  In 1905 Local chieftain <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sultan_Yambio&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sultan Yambio (page does not exist)">Sultan Yambio</a> reluctant to the end gave up the struggle with British forces that had occupied the <a href="/wiki/Kordofan" title="Kordofan">Kordofan</a> region, finally ending the lawlessness. The continued British administration of Sudan fuelled an increasingly strident nationalist backlash, with Egyptian nationalist leaders determined to force Britain to recognise a single independent union of Egypt and Sudan. With a formal end to Ottoman rule in 1914, Sir <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Wingate" title="Reginald Wingate">Reginald Wingate</a> was sent that December to occupy Sudan as the new Military Governor. <a href="/wiki/Hussein_Kamel_of_Egypt" title="Hussein Kamel of Egypt">Hussein Kamel</a> was declared <a href="/wiki/Sultan_of_Egypt_and_Sudan" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultan of Egypt and Sudan">Sultan of Egypt and Sudan</a>, as was his brother and successor, <a href="/wiki/Fuad_I_of_Egypt" title="Fuad I of Egypt">Fuad I</a>. They continued upon their insistence of a single Egyptian-Sudanese state even when the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Egypt" title="Sultanate of Egypt">Sultanate of Egypt</a> was retitled as the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Kingdom of Egypt">Kingdom of Egypt and Sudan</a>, but it was <a href="/wiki/Saad_Zaghloul" title="Saad Zaghloul">Saad Zaghloul</a> who continued to be frustrated in the ambitions until his death in 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan_camel_soldier_of_the_British_army.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan_camel_soldier_of_the_British_army.jpg/220px-Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan_camel_soldier_of_the_British_army.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan_camel_soldier_of_the_British_army.jpg/330px-Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan_camel_soldier_of_the_British_army.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan_camel_soldier_of_the_British_army.jpg/440px-Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan_camel_soldier_of_the_British_army.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2742" data-file-height="2318" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan_camel_soldier_of_the_British_army.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A camel soldier of the native forces of the British army, early 20th century.</div></div></div>
<p>From 1924 until independence in 1956, the British had a policy of running Sudan as two essentially separate territories, the north and south. The <a href="/wiki/Lee_Stack" title="Lee Stack">assassination of a Governor-General of Anglo-Egyptian Sudan in Cairo</a> was the causative factor; it brought demands of the newly elected <a href="/wiki/Wafd_Party" title="Wafd Party">Wafd</a> government from colonial forces. A permanent establishment of two battalions in Khartoum was renamed the <a href="/wiki/Sudan_Defence_Force" title="Sudan Defence Force">Sudan Defence Force</a> acting as under the government, replacing the former garrison of Egyptian army soldiers, saw action afterwards during the <a href="/wiki/Abyssinia_Crisis" title="Abyssinia Crisis">Walwal Incident</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wafdist" class="mw-redirect" title="Wafdist">Wafdist</a> parliamentary majority had rejected <a href="/wiki/Abdel_Khalek_Sarwat_Pasha" title="Abdel Khalek Sarwat Pasha">Sarwat Pasha</a>’s accommodation plan with <a href="/wiki/Austen_Chamberlain" title="Austen Chamberlain">Austen Chamberlain</a> in London; yet Cairo still needed the money. The Sudanese Government's revenue had reached a peak in 1928 at £6.6 million, thereafter the Wafdist disruptions, and Italian borders incursions from Somaliland, London decided to reduce expenditure during the Great Depression. Cotton and gum exports were dwarfed by the necessity to import almost everything from Britain leading to a balance of payments deficit at Khartoum.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 1936 the Liberal Constitutional leader, Muhammed Mahmoud was persuaded to bring Wafd delegates to London to sign the Anglo-Egyptian Treaty, "the beginning of a new stage in Anglo-Egyptian relations", wrote <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Eden" title="Anthony Eden">Anthony Eden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The British Army was allowed to return to Sudan to protect the Canal Zone. They were able to find training facilities, and the RAF was free to fly over Egyptian territory. It did not, however, resolve the problem of Sudan: the Sudanese Intelligentsia agitated for a return to metropolitan rule, conspiring with Germany's agents.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mussolini made it clear that he could not invade Abyssinia without first conquering Egypt and Sudan; they intended unification of Libya with Italian East Africa. The British Imperial General Staff prepared for military defense of the region, which was thin on the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The British ambassador blocked Italian attempts to secure a Non-Aggression Treaty with Egypt-Sudan. But Mahmoud was a supporter of the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Mufti_of_Jerusalem" title="Grand Mufti of Jerusalem">Grand Mufti of Jerusalem</a>; the region was caught between the Empire's efforts to save the Jews, and moderate Arab calls to halt migration.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sudanese Government was directly involved militarily in the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Campaign_(World_War_II)" class="mw-redirect" title="East African Campaign (World War II)">East African Campaign</a>. Formed in 1925, the <a href="/wiki/Sudan_Defence_Force" title="Sudan Defence Force">Sudan Defence Force</a> played an active part in responding to incursions early in World War Two.  Italian troops occupied <a href="/wiki/Kassala" title="Kassala">Kassala</a> and other border areas from <a href="/wiki/Italian_East_Africa" title="Italian East Africa">Italian Somaliland</a> during 1940.  In 1942, the SDF also played a part in the invasion of the Italian colony by British and Commonwealth forces.  The last British <a href="/wiki/Governor-general" title="Governor-general">governor-general</a> was <a href="/wiki/Robert_George_Howe" title="Robert George Howe">Robert George Howe</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_revolution_of_1952" title="Egyptian revolution of 1952">Egyptian revolution of 1952</a> finally heralded the beginning of the march towards Sudanese independence. Having abolished the monarchy in 1953, Egypt's new leaders, <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Naguib" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohammed Naguib">Mohammed Naguib</a>, whose mother was Sudanese, and later <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>, believed the only way to end British domination in Sudan was for Egypt to officially abandon its claims of sovereignty. In addition Nasser knew it would be difficult for Egypt to govern an impoverished Sudan after its independence. The British on the other hand continued their political and financial support for the Mahdist successor, <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Rahman_al-Mahdi" title="Abd al-Rahman al-Mahdi">Abd al-Rahman al-Mahdi</a>, whom it was believed would resist Egyptian pressure for Sudanese independence.  Rahman was capable of this, but his regime was plagued by political ineptitude, which garnered a colossal loss of support in northern and central Sudan. Both Egypt and Britain sensed a great instability fomenting, and thus opted to allow both Sudanese regions, north and south to have a free vote on whether they wished independence or a British withdrawal.
</p>
<h3><span id="Independence_.281956.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_(1956–present)">Independence (1956–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Independence (1956–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sudan_(1956%E2%80%9369)" title="History of Sudan (1956–69)">History of Sudan (1956–69)</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sudan_(1969%E2%80%9385)" title="History of Sudan (1969–85)">History of Sudan (1969–85)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sudan_(1986%E2%80%93present)" title="History of Sudan (1986–present)">History of Sudan (1986–present)</a></div>
<table class="box-Missing_information plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w.svg" class="image"><img alt="Wiki letter w.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6c/Wiki_letter_w.svg/44px-Wiki_letter_w.svg.png" decoding="async" width="44" height="44" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6c/Wiki_letter_w.svg/66px-Wiki_letter_w.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6c/Wiki_letter_w.svg/88px-Wiki_letter_w.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="44" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>is missing information about the history of Sudan between 1956 and 1969 and between 1977 and 1989</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please expand the section to include this information. Further details may exist on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Sudan" title="Talk:Sudan">talk page</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2016</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sudan_independence_2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Sudan_independence_2.png/300px-Sudan_independence_2.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Sudan_independence_2.png/450px-Sudan_independence_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Sudan_independence_2.png/600px-Sudan_independence_2.png 2x" data-file-width="638" data-file-height="441" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sudan_independence_2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sudan's flag raised at independence ceremony on 1 January 1956 by the Prime Minister Ismail al-Azhari and in presence of opposition leader Mohamed Ahmed Almahjoub</div></div></div> 
<p>A polling process was carried out resulting in composition of a democratic parliament and <a href="/wiki/Ismail_al-Azhari" title="Ismail al-Azhari">Ismail al-Azhari</a> was elected first Prime Minister and led the first modern Sudanese government.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> On 1 January 1956, in a special ceremony held at the People's Palace, the Egyptian and British flags were lowered and the new Sudanese flag, composed of green, blue and yellow stripes, was raised in their place by the prime minister <a href="/wiki/Ismail_al-Azhari" title="Ismail al-Azhari">Ismail al-Azhari</a>.
</p><p>Dissatisfaction culminated in a second <a href="/wiki/1969_Sudanese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1969 Sudanese coup d&#39;état">coup d'état</a> on 25 May 1969. The coup leader, Col. <a href="/wiki/Gaafar_Nimeiry" title="Gaafar Nimeiry">Gaafar Nimeiry</a>, became prime minister, and the new regime abolished parliament and outlawed all political parties. Disputes between <a href="/wiki/Marxist" class="mw-redirect" title="Marxist">Marxist</a> and non-Marxist elements within the ruling military coalition resulted in <a href="/wiki/1971_Sudanese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1971 Sudanese coup d&#39;état">a briefly successful coup in July 1971</a>, led by the <a href="/wiki/Sudanese_Communist_Party" title="Sudanese Communist Party">Sudanese Communist Party</a>. Several days later, anti-communist military elements restored Nimeiry to power.
In 1972, the <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa_Agreement_(1972)" title="Addis Ababa Agreement (1972)">Addis Ababa Agreement</a> led to a cessation of the north-south civil war and a degree of self-rule. This led to ten years hiatus in the civil war but an end to American investment in the <a href="/wiki/Jonglei_Canal" title="Jonglei Canal">Jonglei Canal</a> project.  This had been considered absolutely essential to irrigate the Upper Nile region and to prevent an environmental catastrophe and wide-scale famine among the local tribes, most especially the Dinka. In the civil war that followed their homeland was raided looted, pillaged and burned.  Many of the tribe were murdered in a bloody civil war that raged for over 20 years.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hashem_al_Atta,_1971_Sudanese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Hashem_al_Atta%2C_1971_Sudanese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat.jpg/220px-Hashem_al_Atta%2C_1971_Sudanese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Hashem_al_Atta%2C_1971_Sudanese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat.jpg/330px-Hashem_al_Atta%2C_1971_Sudanese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Hashem_al_Atta%2C_1971_Sudanese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat.jpg/440px-Hashem_al_Atta%2C_1971_Sudanese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1005" data-file-height="625" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hashem_al_Atta,_1971_Sudanese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/1971_Sudanese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1971 Sudanese coup d&#39;état">1971 Sudanese coup d'état</a></div></div></div>
<p>Until the early 1970s, Sudan's agricultural output was mostly dedicated to internal consumption. In 1972, the Sudanese government became more pro-Western, and made plans to export food and <a href="/wiki/Cash_crop" title="Cash crop">cash crops</a>. However, commodity prices declined throughout the 1970s causing economic problems for Sudan. At the same time, debt servicing costs, from the money spent mechanizing agriculture, rose. In 1978, the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">IMF</a> negotiated a <a href="/wiki/Structural_Adjustment_Program" class="mw-redirect" title="Structural Adjustment Program">Structural Adjustment Program</a> with the government. This further promoted the mechanised export agriculture sector. This caused great hardship for the pastoralists of Sudan (see <a href="/wiki/Nuba_peoples#Effect_of_private_agriculture_schemes" title="Nuba peoples">Nuba peoples</a>).  In 1976, the Ansars had mounted a bloody but unsuccessful coup attempt. But in July 1977, President Nimeiry met with Ansar leader <a href="/wiki/Sadiq_al-Mahdi" title="Sadiq al-Mahdi">Sadiq al-Mahdi</a>, opening the way for a possible reconciliation.  Hundreds of political prisoners were released, and in August a general amnesty was announced for all oppositionists.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1989-2019_Bashir_government">1989-2019 Bashir government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1989-2019 Bashir government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Omar_al-Bashir_(2017-11-23)_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Omar_al-Bashir_%282017-11-23%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/300px-Omar_al-Bashir_%282017-11-23%29_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Omar_al-Bashir_%282017-11-23%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/450px-Omar_al-Bashir_%282017-11-23%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Omar_al-Bashir_%282017-11-23%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/600px-Omar_al-Bashir_%282017-11-23%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1161" data-file-height="1160" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Omar_al-Bashir_(2017-11-23)_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Omar al-Bashir in 2017</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/1989_Sudanese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1989 Sudanese coup d&#39;état">1989 Sudanese coup d'état</a></div>
<p>On 30 June 1989, Colonel <a href="/wiki/Omar_al-Bashir" title="Omar al-Bashir">Omar al-Bashir</a> led a bloodless <a href="/wiki/1989_Sudanese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1989 Sudanese coup d&#39;état">military coup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-reuters-factbox_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters-factbox-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The new military government suspended political parties and introduced an Islamic legal code on the national level.<sup id="cite_ref-Bekele_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bekele-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Later al-Bashir carried out purges and executions in the upper ranks of the army, the banning of associations, political parties, and independent newspapers, and the imprisonment of leading political figures and journalists.<sup id="cite_ref-Kepel,_Jihad_2002,_p.181_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kepel,_Jihad_2002,_p.181-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> On 16 October 1993, al-Bashir appointed himself "<a href="/wiki/President_of_Sudan" class="mw-redirect" title="President of Sudan">President</a>" and disbanded the Revolutionary Command Council. The executive and legislative powers of the council were taken by al-Bashir.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Sudanese_general_election,_1996" class="mw-redirect" title="Sudanese general election, 1996">1996 general election</a>, he was the only candidate by law to run for election.<sup id="cite_ref-New_York_Times_1996,_p.4_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_York_Times_1996,_p.4-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Sudan became a <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">one-party state</a> under the <a href="/wiki/National_Congress_(Sudan)" title="National Congress (Sudan)">National Congress Party</a> (NCP).<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> During the 1990s, <a href="/wiki/Hassan_al-Turabi" class="mw-redirect" title="Hassan al-Turabi">Hassan al-Turabi</a>, then Speaker of the National Assembly, reached out to <a href="/wiki/Islamic_fundamentalism" title="Islamic fundamentalism">Islamic fundamentalist</a> groups, invited <a href="/wiki/Osama_bin_Laden" title="Osama bin Laden">Osama bin Laden</a> to the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Shahzad_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shahzad-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The United States subsequently listed Sudan as a <a href="/wiki/State_Sponsors_of_Terrorism" class="mw-redirect" title="State Sponsors of Terrorism">state sponsor of terrorism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-foxnews_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foxnews-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Following Al Qaeda's <a href="/wiki/1998_United_States_embassy_bombings" title="1998 United States embassy bombings">bombing of the U.S. embassies in Kenya and Tanzania</a> the U.S. launched <a href="/wiki/Operation_Infinite_Reach" title="Operation Infinite Reach">Operation Infinite Reach</a> and targeted the <a href="/wiki/Al-Shifa_pharmaceutical_factory" title="Al-Shifa pharmaceutical factory">Al-Shifa pharmaceutical factory</a> which the U.S. government falsely believed was producing chemical weapons for the terrorist group. Al-Turabi's influence began to wane, others in favour of more pragmatic leadership tried to change Sudan's <a href="/wiki/International_isolation" title="International isolation">international isolation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The country worked to appease its critics by expelling members of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Islamic_Jihad" title="Egyptian Islamic Jihad">Egyptian Islamic Jihad</a> and encouraging bin Laden to leave.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
