<td style="text-align:right;">39.6
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In Sudan's 2008 census, the population of Northern, Western and Eastern Sudan was recorded to be over 30 million.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> This puts present estimates of the population of Sudan after the secession of <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> at a little over 30 million people. This is a significant increase over the past two decades as the 1983 census put the total population of Sudan, including present-day South Sudan, at 21.6 million.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> The population of Greater Khartoum (including <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omdurman" title="Omdurman">Omdurman</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Khartoum_North" title="Khartoum North">Khartoum North</a>) is growing rapidly and was recorded to be 5.2 million.
</p><p>Despite being a refugee-generating country, Sudan also hosts a refugee population. According to the <i>World Refugee Survey 2008</i>, published by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Committee_for_Refugees_and_Immigrants" title="U.S. Committee for Refugees and Immigrants">U.S. Committee for Refugees and Immigrants</a>, 310,500 refugees and asylum seekers lived in Sudan in 2007. The majority of this population came from <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> (240,400 people), <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> (45,000), <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> (49,300) and the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a> (2,500).<sup id="cite_ref-World_Refugee_Survey_2008_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Refugee_Survey_2008-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> The Sudanese government <a href="/wiki/UN_High_Commissioner_for_Refugees" class="mw-redirect" title="UN High Commissioner for Refugees">UN High Commissioner for Refugees</a> in 2007 forcibly deported at least 1,500 refugees and asylum seekers during the year. Sudan is a party to the 1951 <a href="/wiki/Convention_Relating_to_the_Status_of_Refugees" title="Convention Relating to the Status of Refugees">Convention Relating to the Status of Refugees</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-World_Refugee_Survey_2008_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Refugee_Survey_2008-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Ethnic groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisa_shikawi.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b3/Eisa_shikawi.JPG/220px-Eisa_shikawi.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b3/Eisa_shikawi.JPG/330px-Eisa_shikawi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b3/Eisa_shikawi.JPG/440px-Eisa_shikawi.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1067" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisa_shikawi.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sudanese_Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Sudanese Arab">Sudanese Arab</a> of <a href="/wiki/Manasir" title="Manasir">Al-Manasir</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sudanese_Arabs" title="Sudanese Arabs">Arab</a> presence is estimated at 53% of the Sudanese population.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Others include the Arabised ethnic groups of <a href="/wiki/Nubian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Nubian people">Nubians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaghawa_people" title="Zaghawa people">Zaghawa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Copts_in_Sudan" title="Copts in Sudan">Copts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-unhcr1_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unhcr1-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sudan has 597 groups that speak over 400 different languages and dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sudanese_Arabs" title="Sudanese Arabs">Sudanese Arabs</a> are by far the largest ethnic group in Sudan. They are almost entirely Muslims; while the majority speak <a href="/wiki/Sudanese_Arabic" title="Sudanese Arabic">Sudanese Arabic</a>, some other Arab tribes speak different Arabic dialects like <a href="/wiki/Awadia_and_Fadnia_tribes" title="Awadia and Fadnia tribes">Awadia and Fadnia tribes</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Arakieen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Arakieen (page does not exist)">Bani Arak</a> tribes who speak <a href="/wiki/Najdi_Arabic" title="Najdi Arabic">Najdi Arabic</a>; and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rufa%27a&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Rufa&#39;a (page does not exist)">Rufa'a</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beni_%E1%B8%A4ass%C4%81n" title="Beni Ḥassān">Beni Ḥassān</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Ashraf" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Ashraf">Al-Ashraf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kinanah" class="mw-redirect" title="Kinanah">Kinanah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rashaida" class="mw-redirect" title="Rashaida">Rashaida</a> who speak <a href="/wiki/Hejazi_Arabic" title="Hejazi Arabic">Hejazi Arabic</a>. In addition, the Western province comprises various ethnic groups, while a few Arab <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> of the northern <a href="/wiki/Rizeigat" class="mw-redirect" title="Rizeigat">Rizeigat</a> and others who speak Sudanese Arabic share the same culture and backgrounds of the Sudanese Arabs.
</p><p>The majority of Arabised and indigenous tribes like the <a href="/wiki/Fur_people" title="Fur people">Fur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaghawa_people" title="Zaghawa people">Zaghawa</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Borgo_people&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Borgo people (page does not exist)">Borgo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Masalit_people" title="Masalit people">Masalit</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Baggara" title="Baggara">Baggara</a> ethnic groups, who speak <a href="/wiki/Chadian_Arabic" title="Chadian Arabic">Chadian Arabic</a>, show less cultural integration because of cultural, linguistic and genealogical variations with other Arab and Arabised tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sudanese Arabs of Northern and Eastern parts descend primarily from migrants from the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> and intermarriages with the pre-existing indigenous populations of Sudan, especially the <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubian people</a>, who also share a common history with <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. Additionally, a few pre-Islamic Arabian tribes existed in Sudan from earlier migrations into the region from Western Arabia, although most Arabs in Sudan are dated from migrations after the 12th century.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The vast majority of Arab tribes in Sudan migrated into the Sudan in the 12th century, intermarried with the indigenous Nubian and other African populations and introduced Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-google16_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google16-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sudan consists of numerous other non-Arabic groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/Masalit_people" title="Masalit people">Masalit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaghawa_people" title="Zaghawa people">Zaghawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fulani" class="mw-redirect" title="Fulani">Fulani</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_Nubians&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Northern Nubians (page does not exist)">Northern Nubians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuba" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuba">Nuba</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Beja_people" title="Beja people">Beja people</a>.
</p><p>There is also a small, but prominent <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Sudan" title="Greeks in Sudan">Greek community</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Sudan" title="Languages of Sudan">Languages of Sudan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rashaida_family.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Rashaida_family.png/200px-Rashaida_family.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Rashaida_family.png/300px-Rashaida_family.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Rashaida_family.png/400px-Rashaida_family.png 2x" data-file-width="1419" data-file-height="1671" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rashaida_family.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Arabic-speaking <a href="/wiki/Rashaida_people" title="Rashaida people">Rashaida</a> came to Sudan from <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> about 90 years ago.</div></div></div>
<p>Approximately 70 languages are native to Sudan.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sudanese_Arabic" title="Sudanese Arabic">Sudanese Arabic</a> is the most widely spoken language in the country. It is the <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic">variety of Arabic</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic</a> language of the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a> branch spoken throughout Sudan. The dialect has borrowed much vocabulary from local Nilo-Saharan languages (<a href="/wiki/Nobiin_language" title="Nobiin language">Nobiin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fur_language" title="Fur language">Fur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaghawa_language" title="Zaghawa language">Zaghawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maba_language" title="Maba language">Mabang</a>). This has resulted in a variety of Arabic that is unique to Sudan, reflecting the way in which the country has been influenced by Nilotic, Arab, and western cultures. Few nomads in Sudan still have similar accents to the ones in <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hausa_language" title="Hausa language">Hausa</a> Language is from the Afroasiatic family and is the second most spoken language in Sudan with more than 8 million speakers. Other important languages include <a href="/wiki/Beja_language" title="Beja language">Beja</a> (Bedawi) along the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>, with perhaps two million speakers. It is the language from the Afroasiatic family's <a href="/wiki/Cushitic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Cushitic language">Cushitic</a> branch that is today spoken in the territory. The second most spoken language in eastern Sudan is the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tigryit&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tigryit (page does not exist)">Tigryit</a> also known as <a href="/wiki/Tigre_language" title="Tigre language">Tigre</a>, spoken by the other portion of the <a href="/wiki/Beja_people" title="Beja people">Beja</a>, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bani-amir&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bani-amir (page does not exist)">Bani-amir</a> and other small <a href="/wiki/Cushitic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cushitic">Cushitic</a> tribes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>As with South Sudan, a number of <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilo-Saharan languages</a> are also spoken in Sudan. <a href="/wiki/Fur_language" title="Fur language">Fur</a> speakers inhabit the west (<a href="/wiki/Darfur" title="Darfur">Darfur</a>), with perhaps a million speakers. There are likewise various <a href="/wiki/Nubian_languages" title="Nubian languages">Nubian languages</a>, with over 6 million speakers along the Nile in the north. The most linguistically diverse region in the country is the <a href="/wiki/Nuba_Hills" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuba Hills">Nuba Hills</a> area in Kordofan, inhabited by speakers of multiple language families, with Darfur and other border regions being second.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Niger%E2%80%93Congo_languages" title="Niger–Congo languages">Niger–Congo</a> family is represented by many of the <a href="/wiki/Kordofanian_languages" title="Kordofanian languages">Kordofanian languages</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> by <a href="/wiki/Domari_language" title="Domari language">Domari</a> (Gypsy) and English. Historically, <a href="/wiki/Old_Nubian" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Nubian">Old Nubian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a> were the languages of <a href="/wiki/Christian_Nubia" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian Nubia">Christian Nubia</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Meroitic_language" title="Meroitic language">Meroitic</a> was the language of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kingdom of Kush</a>, which conquered Egypt.
</p><p>Sudan also has multiple regional sign languages, which are not <a href="/wiki/Mutually_intelligible" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutually intelligible">mutually intelligible</a>. A 2009 proposal for a unified <a href="/wiki/Sudanese_Sign_Language" class="mw-redirect" title="Sudanese Sign Language">Sudanese Sign Language</a> had been worked out, but was not widely known.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Prior to 2005, Arabic was the nation's sole <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LeClerc_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LeClerc-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> In the 2005 constitution, Sudan's official languages became Arabic and English.<sup id="cite_ref-Const_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Const-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_areas">Urban areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Urban areas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Sudan" title="Religion in Sudan">Religion in Sudan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Masjid_Al-Nilin,_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Masjid_Al-Nilin%2C_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg/300px-Masjid_Al-Nilin%2C_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Masjid_Al-Nilin%2C_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg/450px-Masjid_Al-Nilin%2C_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Masjid_Al-Nilin%2C_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg/600px-Masjid_Al-Nilin%2C_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="525" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Masjid_Al-Nilin,_August_2007_-_by_Nick_Hobgood.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Masjid Al-Nilin, August 2007</div></div></div>
<div class="barbox tright" style="overflow-x: auto;">
<div style="border:1px solid silver; font-size:88%; padding:0.4em; width:auto; background: white;">
<table style="text-align:left; border-collapse:collapse; width:100%;">
<tbody><tr style="background:#ddd"><th style="text-align:center;" colspan="5">Religion in Sudan<sup id="cite_ref-CWF_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CWF-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup></th></tr>
<tr style="font-size:88%; height:4px;">
<td style="padding:0 4px; text-align:left;">religion</td>

</tbody></table>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the 2011 division which split off South Sudan, over 97% of the population in the remaining Sudan adheres to Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-Islam_in_Sudan1_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Islam_in_Sudan1-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> Most Muslims are divided between two groups: <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> and Salafi (Ansar Al Sunnah) Muslims. Two popular divisions of Sufism, the Ansar and the Khatmia, are associated with the opposition Umma and Democratic Unionist parties, respectively. Only the Darfur region has traditionally been bereft of the Sufi brotherhoods common in the rest of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Roman Catholics are 3.2% of the population and Protestants are 5% mainly in the south. Significant, long-established groups of <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Orthodox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox</a> Christians exist in Khartoum and other northern cities. <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Ethiopian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eritrean_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Eritrean Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Eritrean Orthodox</a> communities also exist in Khartoum and eastern Sudan, largely made up of refugees and migrants from the past few decades. The largest groups affiliated with Western Christian denominations are <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican</a>. Other Christian groups with smaller followings in the country include the Africa Inland Church, the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Apostolic Church</a>, the Sudan Church of Christ, the <a href="/wiki/Sudan_Interior_Church" title="Sudan Interior Church">Sudan Interior Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah&#39;s Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a>, the Sudan Pentecostal Church, the <a href="/wiki/Sudan_Evangelical_Presbyterian_Church" title="Sudan Evangelical Presbyterian Church">Sudan Evangelical Presbyterian Church</a> (in the North).
</p><p>Religious identity plays a role in the country's political divisions. Northern and western Muslims have dominated the country's political and economic system since independence. The NCP draws much of its support from <a href="/wiki/Islamists" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamists">Islamists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salafis" class="mw-redirect" title="Salafis">Salafis</a>/<a href="/wiki/Wahhabis" class="mw-redirect" title="Wahhabis">Wahhabis</a> and other conservative Arab Muslims in the north. The <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Umma</a> Party has traditionally attracted Arab followers of the Ansar sect of Sufism as well as non-Arab Muslims from Darfur and Kordofan. The Democratic Unionist Party (DUP) includes both Arab and non-Arab Muslims in the north and east, especially those in the Khatmia Sufi sect.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Sudan" title="Music of Sudan">Music of Sudan</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Sudanese_writers" title="List of Sudanese writers">List of Sudanese writers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Sudanese_singers" title="List of Sudanese singers">List of Sudanese singers</a></div>
<p>Sudanese culture melds the behaviors, practices, and beliefs of about 578 ethnic groups, communicating in 145 different languages, in a region microcosmic of Africa, with geographic extremes varying from sandy desert to tropical forest.
Recent evidence suggests that while most citizens of the country identify strongly with both Sudan and their religion, Arab and African supranational identities are much more polarising and contested.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Sudan" title="Music of Sudan">Music of Sudan</a></div> 
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_(8625532075).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_%288625532075%29.jpg/220px-Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_%288625532075%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_%288625532075%29.jpg/330px-Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_%288625532075%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_%288625532075%29.jpg/440px-Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_%288625532075%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Drummer_at_Hamed_el-Nil_Mosque_(8625532075).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Sufi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> <a href="/wiki/Dervish" title="Dervish">dervish</a> drums up the Friday afternoon crowd in <a href="/wiki/Omdurman" title="Omdurman">Omdurman</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Sudan has a rich and unique musical culture that has been through chronic instability and repression during the modern history of Sudan. Beginning with the imposition of strict <a href="/wiki/Salafi" class="mw-redirect" title="Salafi">Salafi</a> interpretation of <i><a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a></i> law in 1989, many of the country's most prominent poets, like <a href="/wiki/Mahjoub_Sharif" title="Mahjoub Sharif">Mahjoub Sharif</a>, were imprisoned while others, like <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mohammed_el_Amin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mohammed el Amin (page does not exist)">Mohammed el Amin</a> (returned to Sudan in the mid-1990s) and <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Wardi" title="Mohammed Wardi">Mohammed Wardi</a> (returned to Sudan 2003), fled to Cairo. Traditional music suffered too, with traditional <a href="/wiki/Z%C4%81r" title="Zār">Zār</a> ceremonies being interrupted and drums confiscated <sup class="reference" id="ref_roughguidea"><a href="#endnote_roughguidea">[1]</a></sup>. At the same time European militaries contributed to the development of Sudanese music by introducing new instruments and styles; military bands, especially the Scottish <a href="/wiki/Bagpipes" title="Bagpipes">bagpipes</a>, were renowned, and set traditional music to <a href="/wiki/March_(music)" title="March (music)">military march</a> music. The march <i>March Shulkawi No 1</i>, is an example, set to the sounds of the <a href="/wiki/Shilluk_people" title="Shilluk people">Shilluk</a>. Northern Sudan listens to different music than the rest of Sudan. A type of music called Aldlayib uses a musical instrument called the Tambur. The Tambur has five strings and is made from wood and makes music accompanied by the voices of human applause and singing artists. This music has a perfect blend that gives the area of the Northern State a special character.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The most popular sports in Sudan are athletics (<a href="/wiki/Track_and_field" title="Track and field">track and field</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a>. Though not as successful as football, <a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">basketball</a>, <a href="/wiki/Team_handball" class="mw-redirect" title="Team handball">handball</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Volleyball" title="Volleyball">volleyball</a> are also popular in Sudan. In the 1960s and 1970s, the <a href="/wiki/Sudan_national_basketball_team" title="Sudan national basketball team">national basketball team</a> finished among the continent's top teams. Nowadays, it is only a minor force.
</p><p>Sudanese football has a long history. Sudan was one of the four African nations – the others being Egypt, Ethiopia and South Africa – which formed African football. Sudan hosted the first <a href="/wiki/African_Cup_of_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="African Cup of Nations">African Cup of Nations</a> in 1956, and has won the African Cup Of Nations once, in 1970. Two years later, the Sudan's National Football Team participated in the <a href="/wiki/1972_Summer_Olympics" title="1972 Summer Olympics">1972 Olympic Games</a> in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">nation's capital</a> is home to the Khartoum League, which is considered to be the oldest football league in Africa.
</p><p>Sudanese football teams such as <a href="/wiki/Al-Hilal_Club_(Omdurman)" title="Al-Hilal Club (Omdurman)">Al-Hilal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Merrikh_SC" title="Al-Merrikh SC">Al-Merrikh</a>, and Abdelgadir Osman FC are among the nation's strongest teams. Other teams like Khartoum, El-Neel, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Nidal_El-Nahud&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Al-Nidal El-Nahud (page does not exist)">Al-Nidal El-Nahud</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hay-Al_Arab&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hay-Al Arab (page does not exist)">Hay-Al Arab</a>, are also starting to grow in popularity.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clothing">Clothing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Clothing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most individual Sudanese wear either traditional or western attire. A traditional garb widely worn in Sudan is the <a href="/wiki/Jalabiya" class="mw-redirect" title="Jalabiya">jalabiya</a>, which is a loose-fitting, long-sleeved, collarless ankle-length garment also common to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. The jalabiya is accompanied by a large scarf worn by women, and the garment may be white, colored, striped, and made of fabric varying in thickness, depending on the season of the year and personal preferences.
</p><p>A similar garment common to Sudan is the <i>thobe</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Thawb" title="Thawb">thawb</a></i>, pronounced <i>tobe</i> in Sudanese dialect. The thobe is a long one piece cloth that women wrap around their inner garments. The word "thawb" means "garment" in Arabic.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Sudan" title="Education in Sudan">Education in Sudan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Faculty_of_Science_(University_of_Khartoum)_002.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Faculty_of_Science_%28University_of_Khartoum%29_002.jpg/300px-Faculty_of_Science_%28University_of_Khartoum%29_002.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Faculty_of_Science_%28University_of_Khartoum%29_002.jpg/450px-Faculty_of_Science_%28University_of_Khartoum%29_002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Faculty_of_Science_%28University_of_Khartoum%29_002.jpg/600px-Faculty_of_Science_%28University_of_Khartoum%29_002.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Faculty_of_Science_(University_of_Khartoum)_002.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Khartoum_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Khartoum University">Khartoum University</a> established in 1902</div></div></div>
<p>Education in Sudan is free and compulsory for children aged 6 to 13 years, although more than 40% of children are not going to schools due to the economic situation. Environmental and social factors also increase the difficulty of getting to school, especially for girls.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Primary education consists of eight years, followed by three years of secondary education. The former educational ladder 6 + 3 + 3 was changed in 1990. The primary language at all levels is Arabic. Schools are concentrated in urban areas; many in the west have been damaged or destroyed by years of civil war. In 2001 the World Bank estimated that primary enrollment was 46 percent of eligible pupils and 21 percent of secondary students. Enrollment varies widely, falling below 20 percent in some provinces. The literacy rate is 70.2% of total population, male: 79.6%, female: 60.8%.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_research">Science and research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Science and research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sudan has around 25–30 universities; instruction is primarily in Arabic. Education at the secondary and university levels has been seriously hampered by the requirement that most males perform military service before completing their education.<sup id="cite_ref-cp_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cp-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> In addition, the "Islamisation" encouraged by president Al-Bashir alienated many researchers: the official language of instruction in universities was changed from English to Arabic and Islamic courses became mandatory. Internal science funding withered.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>, more than 3,000 Sudanese researchers left the country between 2002 and 2014. By 2013, the country had a mere 19 researchers for every 100,000 citizens, or 1/30 the ratio of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, according to the Sudanese National Centre for Research. In 2015, Sudan published only about 500 scientific papers.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_229-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> For comparison, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, a country of similar population size, publishes on the order of 10,000 papers per year.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sudan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Sudan" title="Health in Sudan">Health in Sudan</a></div>
<p>Sudan has a <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> of 64.5 years (62.9 for males and 66.1 for females) according to the latest data for the year 2016 from the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> Infant mortality in 2016 was 44.8 per 1,000.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> estimates that 87% of Sudanese women and girls between the ages of 15 to 49 have had <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a> performed on them.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
